    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<span id="freeTextContainer678007172705474634">Mutter in der Entzugsklinik, Vater mit Assistentin auf Geschäftsreise: Maik Klingenberg wird die großen Ferien allein am Pool der elterlichen Villa verbringen.   <span id="freeText678007172705474634" style="display:none">Mutter in der Entzugsklinik, Vater mit Assistentin auf Geschäftsreise: Maik Klingenberg wird die großen Ferien allein am Pool der elterlichen Villa verbringen. Doch dann kreuzt Tschick auf. Tschick, eigentlich Andrej Tschichatschow, kommt aus einem der Asi-Hochhäuser in Hellersdorf, hat es von der Förderschule irgendwie bis aufs Gymnasium geschafft und wirkt doch nicht gerade wie das Musterbeispiel der Integration. Außerdem hat er einen geklauten Wagen zur Hand. Und damit beginnt eine Reise ohne Karte und Kompass durch die sommerglühende deutsche Provinz, unvergesslich wie die Flussfahrt von Tom Sawyer und Huck Finn.</span>

<a href="/questions/851160-why-is-this-considered-a-good-or-even">Why is this considered a good or even great book? I mean the question seriously, not as sarcasm. I read the book and discovered it was classified YA, which may have influenced my opinion. I thought it was ok, but no more than that. Yet when i think of memoirs I&#39;ve liked i keep going back to this. I would like to know why, from a critical point of view, this book is considered so good. Thanks</a>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I really liked the descriptions of the landscape they were passing through. I thought it gave a great sense of how it felt for them to be out in the w<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I really liked the descriptions of the landscape they were passing through. I thought it gave a great sense of how it felt for them to be out in the world by themselves for the first time, feeling like they were almost on another planet.<br /><br />I also like the way the reader was made to feel a sense of fear of the possibilities the adventurers were too naive to feel.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=european-literature">european-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=german-literature">german-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=germany">germany</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56789747-amalia-gkavea?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2929067069" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14887773103747619352"><i>
  <b>‘’Ever since I was a little boy my father had told me that the world was a bad place. The world is bad and people are bad. Don’t trust anyone, don’t talk to strangers, all of that. My parents drilled that into me. When you watched the local news - people were bad. When you saw primetime investigative shows - people were bad. And maybe it was true, maybe ninety-nine percent of people were bad.’’</b>
</i><br/><br/>The story starts in the police station with a frightened boy, a battered leg and quite a lot of diz</span>
  <span id="freeText14887773103747619352" style="display:none"><i>
  <b>‘’Ever since I was a little boy my father had told me that the world was a bad place. The world is bad and people are bad. Don’t trust anyone, don’t talk to strangers, all of that. My parents drilled that into me. When you watched the local news - people were bad. When you saw primetime investigative shows - people were bad. And maybe it was true, maybe ninety-nine percent of people were bad.’’</b>
</i><br /><br />The story starts in the police station with a frightened boy, a battered leg and quite a lot of dizziness. Just a few days earlier two boys wanted to go to the most important party of the year. An unlikely duo they were. Mike, a boy from a well-to-do family residing in the Berlin suburbs. His mother is an alcoholic. His father is a scum. Andrej, the new student who becomes a target because of his Russian heritage and his silence. However, the question is one: Why did they take the car?<br /><br />Because of teenage love that knows no boundaries and is afraid of nothing. Because our partners in crime had to deliver a precious gift to a very special girl. And then, they needed to enjoy the taste of freedom, the carefree summer in the messy world their families have created. During their eventful road trip, they meet a family that adores trivia and Harry Potter, an old man who reminisces on love and war, a strange girl who lives alone in the middle of nowhere. They roam on streets that still bear the signs of the discrimination between the ‘’West’’ and the ‘’East’’, between the Germans and the foreigners, between the world of the adults and the universe of the children.<br /><br />Wolfgang Herrndorf writes a remarkable journey of two teenage boys who need to overcome obstacles that were created by others. They take the car to see whether anything worthy exists beyond the harsh environment of teachers who seem to have been educated in the Nazi Youth during Hitler’s era, of classmates who have started formulating an excellent textbook on how to become a fully-functioning skinhead, of families that are criminally absent or criminally present. And still, through incidents that are funny and sad and messy, through moments that are heart-pounding fast or the epitome of summer serenity, Herrendorf gives us two boys that are clever and kind and able to find their way. Even in an old, stolen Lada. Rich in beautiful descriptions of the lazy suburbs, written in dialogue and prose that are both hilarious and sharp, Why We Took the Car is one more successful example of European YA Literature. In fact, this is exactly how YA SHOULD be...In the end, this is not a journey about discovering yourself but about discovering the true nature of the ones around you and whether you can trust them or not. Most of the times, the answer is not simple…<br /><br /><i>
  <b>‘’And just imagine: The bugs go to the bug movies! They make movies on their planet and they’re sitting in some bug cinema watching a movie set on Earth - it’s about two kids who steal a car.’’</b>
</i><br /><br />My reviews can also be found on <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://theopinionatedreaderblog.wordpress.com">https://theopinionatedreaderblog.word...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="14887773103747619352" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_752295247" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/752295247" />
    <a title="jv poore" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5157918-jv-poore"><img alt="jv poore" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1570108420p2/5157918.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/752295247?book_show_action=true">Oct 29, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="jv poore" class="user" itemprop="url" name="jv poore" href="/user/show/5157918-jv-poore">jv poore</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780545481809" href="/book/show/17270509-why-we-took-the-car">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5157918-jv-poore?shelf=middle-grade">middle-grade</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5157918-jv-poore?shelf=buried-under-books">buried-under-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5157918-jv-poore?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer752295247" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6661937445235259133">Outwardly, I tout “girl power”. I tell my nieces, and other young ladies, that girls can do anything that boys can do. There is equality. In truth though, I’ve always been a bit jealous of boys. Growing up, it seemed that boys just had it easier, specifically in their friendships. Guys appear to be so comfortable with one and other, in a very real way. Buddies may disagree, argue, and even throw down; but, at the end of the day, the rapport is still strong. I don’t know how realistic my vision o</span>
  <span id="freeText6661937445235259133" style="display:none">Outwardly, I tout “girl power”. I tell my nieces, and other young ladies, that girls can do anything that boys can do. There is equality. In truth though, I’ve always been a bit jealous of boys. Growing up, it seemed that boys just had it easier, specifically in their friendships. Guys appear to be so comfortable with one and other, in a very real way. Buddies may disagree, argue, and even throw down; but, at the end of the day, the rapport is still strong. I don’t know how realistic my vision of “boys being boys” really is, but Why We Took the Car gives me hope that I was spot-on. <br /><br />An unlikely friendship, a stolen jalopy and an impromptu road trip made for an exciting tale. At a blush, Mike &amp; Tschick could not be more different. Tschick, poor, with no discernible adult supervision, whose young life has been filled with alcohol and petty crime, appears to enjoy being alone. Mike, on the opposite end of the spectrum, outwardly has it all. An outsider peering in would see a rich kid, with both parents tucked cozily beneath one very fancy roof. Looks are often deceiving.<br /><br />Mike was more than surprised when bad boy Tschick tries to befriend him, largely because Mike sports a really cool thrift-store jacket, styling a white dragon across the chest. Feigning disinterest does not deter Tschick; he is relentless. Without understanding exactly why, Mike stops ignoring him and a tentative foundation for solidarity begins to take shape. <br /><br />Before he can fully comprehend and digest the potential repercussions; Mike amazes himself by agreeing to a “vacation” with his new pal. Prepared with only a vague plan, a stolen car and a few bucks; the young criminals begin their adventure.<br /><br />Their road trip is packed with colourful characters, mini adventures, and a gradual growth of genuine fondness between the boys. While the encounters are exciting, intriguing and fun; they weren’t my absolute favourite part of the story. Rather, the subtle message that sometimes, the people you choose to love are more important than the ones you are expected to love; resonated with me. Almost as importantly, my ideal “boy friendship” was cemented as the implausible companions slowly reveal secrets truths of challenging lives. Their new bond never breaks, it only grows stronger.<br /><br />This distinctive book was an enjoyable read on a couple of levels. The writing is powerful; when I first started this book, I found myself reading very quickly, because I felt like Mike was talking very fast. It is always delightfully surprising when a book sets my reading pace. A fabulous plot complete with unique characters and raw emotion make this a book that I will highly recommend, and in fact, I’m sure it will be one that I read again.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6661937445235259133" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2891788624" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4420357226388865407">Two windmill fighters set out to find Don Quixote's idealistic spirit, and underneath a heap of outlandishly real crap, they find it!<br/><br/>Well, not quite. But almost. Maik has been taught by his parents that 99% of humanity is bad. And considering his mum is an alcoholic who needs to leave her 14-year-old son for a detox clinic over summer, and that his dad is a bankrupt business man who uses the occasion to leave as well, with his young assistant, they have a point. His teachers have been saying the</span>
  <span id="freeText4420357226388865407" style="display:none">Two windmill fighters set out to find Don Quixote's idealistic spirit, and underneath a heap of outlandishly real crap, they find it!<br /><br />Well, not quite. But almost. Maik has been taught by his parents that 99% of humanity is bad. And considering his mum is an alcoholic who needs to leave her 14-year-old son for a detox clinic over summer, and that his dad is a bankrupt business man who uses the occasion to leave as well, with his young assistant, they have a point. His teachers have been saying the same, as have the news and everyone else around him.<br /><br />When Maik is not invited to his secret crush's summer party, and finds himself in the company of Tschick, his Russian classmate with a dubious family situation and a stolen car, he certainly has no reason to doubt that his parents and teachers are right. The world seems 100% bad at that moment.<br /><br />And off they go, on a week-long road trip. They meet the strangest people, and have the weirdest adventures, ending up injured and caught by the police. And yet, on that road trip, Maik notices, they only meet people of the 1% good kind. If that is not a hopeful message to the world, what could possibly be better?<br /><br />A wonderful, wonderful youth story that deservedly has the reputation of a true "all age" or crossover novel. Recommended to those who like Holden Caulfield and Don Quixote, and to those who still believe that windmills can turn into beautiful dragons if you just see them in the right way! <br /><br />Dragon riders!</span>

<div id="review_1535882679" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1535882679" />
    <a title="Harper" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26805841-harper"><img alt="Harper" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495971923p2/26805841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1535882679?book_show_action=true">Feb 03, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Harper" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Harper" href="/user/show/26805841-harper">Harper</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781922070791" href="/book/show/20456192-why-we-took-the-car">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26805841-harper?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26805841-harper?shelf=favourite-characters">favourite-characters</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26805841-harper?shelf=realistic-teens">realistic-teens</a>,

          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1535882679" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16322865871847058632"><blockquote>
  <i>"i looked up at the stars extending out into incomprehensible infinity and was somehow frightened."</i>
</blockquote><br/><br/>I read a lot of books, especially teen fiction (if there's roadtrip novel out there, I will find it and I will read it) but no novel has wrapped itself around my heart just as much as 'Why We Took The Car' has. I'll admit, it's half past nine at night and I started reading a mere few hours ago, and I've been a sobbing mess for about twenty minutes now. <br/><br/>To be honest, I don't know how exactly to </span>
  <span id="freeText16322865871847058632" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>"i looked up at the stars extending out into incomprehensible infinity and was somehow frightened."</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />I read a lot of books, especially teen fiction (if there's roadtrip novel out there, I will find it and I will read it) but no novel has wrapped itself around my heart just as much as 'Why We Took The Car' has. I'll admit, it's half past nine at night and I started reading a mere few hours ago, and I've been a sobbing mess for about twenty minutes now. <br /><br />To be honest, I don't know how exactly to review this. All I know is, this is one that I'll be thinking of for years to come. This is, by far, the <b>best</b> book I have ever read. Herrndorf pulls off the homosexual subtext really well and honestly, it makes the novel what it is. Though I'm almost three years older than Mike, it's not hard to relate to him and find a part of your fourteen year-old self in a character like him.<br /><br />I highly recommend this to anyone who loves roadtrips, angsty teenagers, stolen cars, and loads of trouble. Actually, everyone should read this. Find yourself a copy and read the hell out of it right now, it'll be one of the best (if not the best) things you'll ever do.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_306980954" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/306980954" />
    <a title="Larou" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7821634-larou"><img alt="Larou" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1329869108p2/7821634.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/306980954?book_show_action=true">Apr 05, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Larou" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Larou" href="/user/show/7821634-larou">Larou</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer306980954" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8476269390226300713">Until not all that long ago, there were basically only two kinds of German-language fiction being published – prettty much every released book fell either into the category of very cerebral, highly modernistic literary fiction, or that of trashy, iredeemably bad pulp, with all the huge middle ground between the two extremes being covered by translated books, mostly (and rather unsurprisingly) from the United States. This has somewhat changed over recent years, and these days you can find German </span>
  <span id="freeText8476269390226300713" style="display:none">Until not all that long ago, there were basically only two kinds of German-language fiction being published – prettty much every released book fell either into the category of very cerebral, highly modernistic literary fiction, or that of trashy, iredeemably bad pulp, with all the huge middle ground between the two extremes being covered by translated books, mostly (and rather unsurprisingly) from the United States. This has somewhat changed over recent years, and these days you can find German genre authors who write for readers with more than the minimum intelligence required to decipher a text, and litfic authors to whom the concept of reading for enjoyment is not utterly alien. And sometimes you get a novel like <i>Tschick</i> (which would have been impossible thirty years ago) that is undoubtedly literary fiction but also is insanely fun to read, enjoyable on all kinds of levels, whether it’s analysis of structure and imagery or or whether it’s being swept away by a ripping good yarn.<br /><br />The main reason for <i>Tschick’s</i> success (artistically as well as commercially – the novel was a bestseller in Germany) lies, I think, in its narrative voice, that of fourteen year old Maik who relates events from a first person view. Maik is your average awkward teenager, thinks of himself as boring and is in love with a girl from his class who barely knows that he exists. But then a new guy is introduced, Tschick, the son of Russian immigrants and very much a social outsider. Rather without intending to, Maik slides into a friendship with Tschick who one day stands at his door with a stolen car, and before Maik quite knows what is happening, the two of them are on their towards Wallachia where supposedly Tschick’s grandfather lives.<br /><br />What then follows is a riff on simultaneously road movie, <i>Bildungsroman</i> and quest romance, a wild and hugely entertaining ride through Eastern Germany in the course of which encounter all sorts of bizarre characters while discovering both themselves and each other. It is a very hard thing to do, but Wolfgang Herrndorf manages to exactly hit the note of a fourteen-year old’s voice, giving his narrator just enough jargon and attitude to make him sound his age, but never so much as to make it come across as obtrusive and unconvincing. The novel is funny without being forced, touching without being sentimental, and while the story unfolds in what is quite recognisably present-day Germany it is never simply realistic (at least not in the sense of depicting things “just as they are” as realism is still most commonly understood). The narrative often has a dreamlike feel about it, sometimes peaking into the outright surreal, but it is always somewhat larger than life, as if common reality was not quite sufficient to hold Maik, Tschick and the people they encounter on their trip.<br /><br />And while Wolfgang Herrndorf does not gloss over the bleakness of the East German Maik and Tschick travers in their stolen car, and does not serve the reader a facile happy ending, when all is said done, <i>Tschick</i> is not only a very funy and highly entertaining novel, but also a rather hopeful one; summarised neatly by the narrator at the end when he reflects that he has always been told how people are bad, and even though this might be true of 99% of everyone, he and Tschick on their journey enountered almost exclusively the one percent that was not bad. There is some melancholy in this, but also no small amount of optimism and I’d wager most readers will close this novel with a smile on their face and think back on it fondly.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8476269390226300713" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer440297511" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6690228328344514624">This was so great a book. I especially liked the Finnish translation though it had some odd moments, but overall - wonderful! Herrndorf writes his main characters so unbelievably real, sarcastically funny and believable as (pre)teens. In a way I saw myself in Maik at that age and I had many hilarious moments with the book because of it.<br/><br/>I wanted to give the book five stars, but to that I would've wanted to know more about Tschick. I think Herrndorf pulled the gay part well, but I just wish he wou</span>
  <span id="freeText6690228328344514624" style="display:none">This was so great a book. I especially liked the Finnish translation though it had some odd moments, but overall - wonderful! Herrndorf writes his main characters so unbelievably real, sarcastically funny and believable as (pre)teens. In a way I saw myself in Maik at that age and I had many hilarious moments with the book because of it.<br /><br />I wanted to give the book five stars, but to that I would've wanted to know more about Tschick. I think Herrndorf pulled the gay part well, but I just wish he would've told about it more - same goes with Tschick as a person too. I do understand that we see things through Maik and if he doesn't know, we don't either. For me it was hard to understand Tschick as he seems to be an auxiliary in the story where he's supposed to be the other main character. The ending was great except for the fact that Herrndorf ditched Tschick too conveniently. I wanted a conclusion of some sort for Maik and Tschick. I wanted them to have a grand finale friendship-wise. I'm sad I didn't get it.<br /><br />Either way, Herrndorf is a talented writer and the text flow is amazing. Hardly ever have I read so great a book for young adults. I want to read more, Mr. Herrndorf!</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer9861446998039904512"> Really enjoyed this German coming of age story - it's well written and Mike the 14-year-old narrator is a really nice guy and totally enjoyable to listen too. I've read a number of these kinds of books as part of my job, and usually enjoy the young persons story about their life, insufferable parents/school/teachers/friends etc, but they are have all been from NZ, Australia, England or the USA, so nice to get an idea of life for European teens. Not a lot different from others - but enough inter</span>
  <span id="freeText9861446998039904512" style="display:none"> Really enjoyed this German coming of age story - it's well written and Mike the 14-year-old narrator is a really nice guy and totally enjoyable to listen too. I've read a number of these kinds of books as part of my job, and usually enjoy the young persons story about their life, insufferable parents/school/teachers/friends etc, but they are have all been from NZ, Australia, England or the USA, so nice to get an idea of life for European teens. Not a lot different from others - but enough interesting things happen that just make it a little bit different too. It's a great yarn about a road trip that goes wrong in many ways, but becomes a life changing adventure that hurtles Mike hilariously along the road to becoming a grown up. I think teens will really enjoy it - will find out when I start asking them to try it!</span>

<div id="review_3053515076" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3053515076" />
    <a title="Vishy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5555413-vishy"><img alt="Vishy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307126420p2/5555413.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3053515076?book_show_action=true">Nov 18, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vishy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vishy" href="/user/show/5555413-vishy">Vishy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780545481809" href="/book/show/17270509-why-we-took-the-car">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3053515076" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10234089550973606948">I was looking for a contemporary German book to read and I was thinking about it when I discovered Wolfgang Herrndorf's 'Tschick'. I got it and read the first page and then I couldn't stop reading. <br/><br/>The story told in 'Tschick' goes like this. Mike Klingenberg is fourteen years old and he is the narrator of the story. At the beginning of the story we find Mike in the hospital. There seem to be police with him too. We wonder why. Mike tells us what happened. Mike is a loner at school and doesn't ha</span>
  <span id="freeText10234089550973606948" style="display:none">I was looking for a contemporary German book to read and I was thinking about it when I discovered Wolfgang Herrndorf's 'Tschick'. I got it and read the first page and then I couldn't stop reading. <br /><br />The story told in 'Tschick' goes like this. Mike Klingenberg is fourteen years old and he is the narrator of the story. At the beginning of the story we find Mike in the hospital. There seem to be police with him too. We wonder why. Mike tells us what happened. Mike is a loner at school and doesn't have many (or rather any) friends. The girl he likes, Tatiana, doesn't know that he exists. Mike is good at some things – he is an ace high-jumper and a wonderful artist – but his talent is not noticed. A new boy called Tschick arrives in school one day. He seems to have a complex background and so everyone including Mike ignores him. At some point, something brings these two together and somehow they embark on a long road trip in an old stolen (or shall we say 'borrowed') car. What happens after that – the amazing adventures they have and the fascinating people they meet and how Mike ends up in the hospital and what happens after that – is narrated in the rest of the story. <br /><br />I loved 'Tschick'. Mike is a wonderful narrator with an original, charming voice, a cool style, a wonderful sense of humour, and speaks his mind and doesn't mince words. The pages flew because I loved the narrator's voice. He made me remember all the great teenage / young narrators that I have encountered in some of my favourite novels, like 'Treasure Island', 'Kidnapped', 'To Kill a Mockingbird', 'The Curious Incident of the Dog in the Night-Time', 'Unhooking the Moon', 'The Pull of Gravity', 'The Fault in Our Stars'. Tschick, after whom the novel is named, is a fascinating character, and is one of the two main characters alongwith Mike. Tschick is quiet, but once we get to know him, we discover that he is cool, wise, is filled with surprises and there is more to him than meets the eye. The Mike–Tschick friendship is one of the most charming friendships that I have encountered in any story. The book is very engaging and fast-paced and there is no word wasted. The ending is beautiful but I can't tell you what happened – you have to read the book yourself and find out. <br /><br />I loved 'Tschick' so much that I wanted to read more books by Wolfgang Herrndorf. When I went and did some research, I discovered that this was his first book which he published when he was forty-five, and it was a runaway bestseller. But tragically, he was diagnosed with an incurable form of brain tumour by that time, and he wrote just one more book called 'Sand' soon after that and died three years later. He just had a three-year literary career. He burned bright like a comet, lighted up millions of readers' hearts, and was gone before they could blink. It was heartbreaking to read. Why do good people always die young? <br /><br />A small observation on the title. The German title of the book is 'Tschick'. The title of the English translation is 'Why We Took the Car'. I hate this modern British practice of changing the title of translated works and trying to summarize the book through the title. So I am sticking to the German title here. I like it more.<br /><br />'Tschick' is one of my favourite books of the year. I am glad it was a bestseller and got many accolades – it deserved every bit of that. I can't wait to read Wolfgang Herrndorf's 'Sand' now. <br /><br />I'll leave you with some of my favourite passages from the book.<br /><br />"It took two hours to reach the very top, but it was worth it. The view looked like a really great postcard. There was a giant wooden cross at the highest point, and below that a little cabin. The entire cabin was covered with carvings. We sat down there and read some of the letters and numbers cut into the wood: CKH 4/23/61, SONNY ’86, HARTMANN 1923. The oldest one we could find was: ANSELM WAIL 1903. Old letters cut into old, dark wood. And then the view and the warm summer air and the scent of hay wafting up from the valleys below. Tschick pulled out a pocketknife and started carving. As we talked and basked in the sun and watched Tschick carve, I kept thinking about the fact that in a hundred years we’d all be dead. Like Anselm Wail was dead. His family was all dead too. His parents were dead, his children were dead, everyone who ever knew him was dead. And if he ever made anything or built anything or left anything behind, it was probably dead as well — destroyed, blown away by two world wars — and the only thing left of Anselm Wail was his name carved in a piece of wood. Why had he carved it there? Maybe he’d been on a road trip, like us. Maybe he’d stolen a car or a carriage or a horse or whatever they had back then and rode around having fun. But whatever it was, it would never again be of interest to anyone because there was nothing left of his fun, of his life, of anything. The only people who would ever know anything at all about Anselm Wail were the people who climbed this mountain. And the same thing would be true of us."<br /><br />"I want to talk to my lawyer. That’s the sentence I probably need to say. It’s the right sentence in the right situation, as everybody knows from watching TV. And it’s easy to say: I want to talk to my lawyer. But they’d probably die laughing. Here’s the problem: I have no idea what this sentence means. If I say I want to talk to my lawyer and they ask me, “Who do you want to talk to? Your lawyer?” what am I supposed to answer? I’ve never seen a lawyer in my life, and I don’t even know what I need one for. I don’t know if there’s a difference between a lawyer and an attorney. Or an attorney general. I guess they’re like judges except on my side. I guess they know a lot more about the law than I do. But I guess pretty much everyone in the room knows more about the law than I do. First and foremost the policemen. And I could ask them."<br /><br />"It’s a little like those mafia movies, when there’s a long silence before one gangster answers another, and they just stare at each other. “Hey!” A minute of silence. “Look me in the eyes!” Five minutes of silence. In regular life that would be stupid. But when you’re in the mafia, it’s not."<br /><br />Have you read 'Tschick'? What do you think about it?</span>
  <a data-text-id="10234089550973606948" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12842772345318274886">Mike Klingenberg and Andrej Tschichatschow a.k.a. Tschick are very unpopular and considered by their peers to be rather weird. When neither is invited to the fabulously popular girl’s birthday party, who Mike is seriously crushing on, both boys decide to forget it all and go on a wild road trip across Germany. They make bad decisions, meet interesting people, and are eventually arrested. What seems like a great story actually falls very short. Though this book won the German Youth Literature Awa</span>
  <span id="freeText12842772345318274886" style="display:none">Mike Klingenberg and Andrej Tschichatschow a.k.a. Tschick are very unpopular and considered by their peers to be rather weird. When neither is invited to the fabulously popular girl’s birthday party, who Mike is seriously crushing on, both boys decide to forget it all and go on a wild road trip across Germany. They make bad decisions, meet interesting people, and are eventually arrested. What seems like a great story actually falls very short. Though this book won the German Youth Literature Award, it was difficult for this reader to understand why. Perhaps the better part of the story was lost in translation, but there were still plenty of rambling, stream of consciousness moments and lots of pointless swearing. The main character, Mike, is very unlikable and has a bitter, sarcastic attitude throughout the book. Part of what makes a story excellent is the protagonist’s growth at the conclusion. In this case, not much growth was visible. Not recommended.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12772146533913463696">Kind of a strange story and I don‘t really see why the writer might have felt compelled to write it. I‘m quite curious though. </span>

          </span>

<div id="review_3314149725" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3314149725?book_show_action=true">May 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephanie Griffin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephanie Griffin" href="/user/show/514234-stephanie-griffin">Stephanie Griffin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780545481809" href="/book/show/17270509-why-we-took-the-car">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/514234-stephanie-griffin?shelf=2020-read-german-books-challenge">2020-read-german-books-challenge</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3314149725" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10605257564824778609">WHY WE TOOK THE CAR is a great story about two fourteen year old loner-type boys who take a road trip in a stolen car. It’s written by Wolfgang Hernndorf, who prematurely passed away in 2013 at only 48. The book won multiple German writing awards.<br/>The main character, Mike Klingenberg, lives in a well-to-do house with all the nice things but behind the scenes his family is very dysfunctional. Tschick, another loner, has a background that is murkier although if he knows how to steal cars at his age</span>
  <span id="freeText10605257564824778609" style="display:none">WHY WE TOOK THE CAR is a great story about two fourteen year old loner-type boys who take a road trip in a stolen car. It’s written by Wolfgang Hernndorf, who prematurely passed away in 2013 at only 48. The book won multiple German writing awards.<br />The main character, Mike Klingenberg, lives in a well-to-do house with all the nice things but behind the scenes his family is very dysfunctional. Tschick, another loner, has a background that is murkier although if he knows how to steal cars at his age, it’s probably not the best.<br />The two teenagers take backroads to remain incognito and run into lots of eccentric people. Along the way there are also several hair-raising events.<br />I really enjoyed the fact that Mike looks at the world’s beauty, albeit in a singular way, and finds things like the Earth’s terra firma wondrous. Mike has no idea what his future will bring, so he’s going along with whatever comes his way. He hasn’t found who he is yet, like most teenagers.<br />I very much recommend this book.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_1076382106" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1076382106" />
    <a title="Pop Bop" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5292621-pop-bop"><img alt="Pop Bop" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1434776015p2/5292621.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1076382106?book_show_action=true">Oct 09, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pop Bop" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pop Bop" href="/user/show/5292621-pop-bop">Pop Bop</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780545481809" href="/book/show/17270509-why-we-took-the-car">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5292621-pop-bop?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1076382106" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5658270240255061136">Smart, Funny, Touching and Refreshing<br/><br/>There are a lot of books out there that are written to sound like they are being narrated by fourteen year olds. When the narrator is a boy the books are often funny stories of school daze pranks, or yearning puppy dog tales of first love, or sports themed. (I'm putting aside fantasy/adventure books or goony farces.) Many of those books are entertaining, and even in some ways instructive, and I'll be happy to keep reading them as long as ambitious and creativ</span>
  <span id="freeText5658270240255061136" style="display:none">Smart, Funny, Touching and Refreshing<br /><br />There are a lot of books out there that are written to sound like they are being narrated by fourteen year olds. When the narrator is a boy the books are often funny stories of school daze pranks, or yearning puppy dog tales of first love, or sports themed. (I'm putting aside fantasy/adventure books or goony farces.) Many of those books are entertaining, and even in some ways instructive, and I'll be happy to keep reading them as long as ambitious and creative authors keep writing them.<br /><br />But very few of the books can hit the rueful, acerbic, innocently world weary, and yet subtly optimistic tone that lies somewhere between funny/antic and dramatic/serious. This book does. Over and over again. Sure, it can be honestly and deeply funny and it can be amusing in a knowing and off hand way. And, it has its share of serious and dramatic moments. But mostly it hews to the middle ground between the two - showing heart, sadness, energy, melancholy, and all of the confused and conflicting feelings and emotions that can flash through a fourteener's mind. (It never "goes big", but that means it never feels phony or manipulative either.)<br /><br />Our narrator, Mike, is a fourteen year old from a pretty dysfunctional family who has had an antic adventure that has more or less run him off the rails. That we start in a police station and that our hero/narrator is in custody, sets the right tone for the reflective monologue that is about to follow. Because the narrative is a monologue, sometimes feeling like it is addressed to us and sometimes feeling more like it is addressed by the speaker to himself, we get an intimate and quicksilver look into the mind of this kid. And he's smart and observant and funny and self-aware in the same measure that he is awkward, dense and oblivious. He is neither too smart and articulate to convince us as a character, nor so out of touch that we lose interest in him. He feels hyper-real and hyper-authentic, which seems to me to be the best you can expect in this kind of book. (You could probably say the same for the unrealistic but hyper-real Germany Mike drives through.)<br /><br />So, lots happens here, or is at least recounted, but in a way nothing immediate happens. Everything has already happened, and Mike is telling us about it. This is tricky stuff, but while some of it is a little authorly and showy, most of it engaging and thought provoking. That's fine by me, and it leads me to feel that this could be a fine choice for a confident, ambitious young reader ready for a crisp and insightful, if somewhat bleak, hero.<br /><br />Please note that I received a free advance ecopy of this book in exchange for a candid review. Apart from that I have no connection at all to either the author or the publisher of this book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5658270240255061136" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1442184450" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1442184450" />
    <a title="Bjorn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19667916-bjorn"><img alt="Bjorn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1367928200p2/19667916.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1442184450?book_show_action=true">Nov 14, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bjorn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bjorn" href="/user/show/19667916-bjorn">Bjorn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19667916-bjorn?shelf=germany">germany</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1442184450" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8721806180481543448">I hate that the publisher makes sure I know the circumstances this book was written under; if I dislike a book written by a dying author, that makes me feel like an asshole. If I like it, I'm forever (or at least for a while) going to wonder how much of that is me projecting what I know of the author onto the text.<br/><br/>That said, I definitely wound up liking this a lot more than I expected. It looks really clichéd - two bored young teenage outsiders from Berlin, a rich dork and a dirt-poor immigrant,</span>
  <span id="freeText8721806180481543448" style="display:none">I hate that the publisher makes sure I know the circumstances this book was written under; if I dislike a book written by a dying author, that makes me feel like an asshole. If I like it, I'm forever (or at least for a while) going to wonder how much of that is me projecting what I know of the author onto the text.<br /><br />That said, I definitely wound up liking this a lot more than I expected. It looks really clichéd - two bored young teenage outsiders from Berlin, a rich dork and a dirt-poor immigrant, decide to just "borrow" an old car and take off on "holiday" for a few weeks. Because why the hell not, mostly. Turns out that the adult world is a strange and incomprehensible place, and it takes more than just knowing which pedals to push to make your way ... Along the way through the former GDR they bump into various strange people. Or at least they think they're strange, but what do they know?<br /><br />Again, yeah, you've seen this plot before, and when it's all narrated by one of the 14-year-olds it makes me even more wary. But I ended up scoffing the whole book down in two long reads, because it's just that well done. There's a hunger, a force behind Herrndorf's writing, a sense of humour that refuses to turn into comedy. The situations the boys find themselves in aren't just wacky adventures, though they initially appear that way when filtered through a not-as-smart-as-he-thinks kid's view. There's a new Europe out there, one which consists of layers of lots of old ones, and it takes more than just a hotwired Lada to understand it. Freedom's just another word for nothing, etc.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8721806180481543448" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1896838947" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1896838947" />
    <a title="Bücherhäschen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21061557-b-cherh-schen"><img alt="Bücherhäschen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590970190p2/21061557.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1896838947?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bücherhäschen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bücherhäschen" href="/user/show/21061557-b-cherh-schen">Bücherhäschen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9783499256356" href="/book/show/13625190-tschick">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21061557-b-cherh-schen?shelf=read-in-school-or-for-university">read-in-school-or-for-university</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1896838947" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6982157216733461981">Tschick a book written by Wolfgang Herrendorf. It tells the story of Maik Klingenberg and Andrej „Tschick“ Tschichatschow, who embark on a car tour through Germany.<br/><br/><br/>CONTENT<br/>Maik does not have friends, girls are not interested in him, you could (as he does himself) call him boring. His parents are richt, but they have other things in mind. His mother is in rehab, because she's an alcoholic, and his father is on holiday with his very young secreatry. <br/>At the beginning of the book, Tschick is transfer</span>
  <span id="freeText6982157216733461981" style="display:none">Tschick a book written by Wolfgang Herrendorf. It tells the story of Maik Klingenberg and Andrej „Tschick“ Tschichatschow, who embark on a car tour through Germany.<br /><br /><br />CONTENT<br />Maik does not have friends, girls are not interested in him, you could (as he does himself) call him boring. His parents are richt, but they have other things in mind. His mother is in rehab, because she's an alcoholic, and his father is on holiday with his very young secreatry. <br />At the beginning of the book, Tschick is transferred to his class. They don't become friends imediately, as Maik does not have that good an opinion of Tschik, who comes to school drunk quite often. When summer starts, they get along quite well and become friends very quickly, which ends in a car tour. On their way, they meet the girl Isa, who lives on a trash dump, and more or less become friends. <br /><br />SOMETHING OVERALL<br />I had to read the book as part of one of my lectures for University- this in particular was about masculinity. You can interpert it that way: Maik and Tschick are on a journey to find their masculinity, but also their identity.<br />Not considering that, the book is interesting enough. It tells the story of two young boys who go on a journey without a specific goal, and have some Kind of everyday adventures. Nonetheless it still is one of those books, I can see a school (or University) class reading, rather than someone buying it for fun.<br /><br />STYLE OF WRITING<br />It's modern. It uses slang, which you can hear on the Underground everyday, without exaggerating. BUT, you have to like that. I, for example, prefer books with whole, grammatically correct sentences. <br />Something else I noticed is, that the book tells us, the main characters are in Gymnasium (Google says it's academic high School in Englisch)- my point is, the slang did not fell genuine for the type of school they attended. (at least in my opinion, and I went to that Kind of school)<br /><br />CHARACTERS<br />Maik is not our typical hero, and he's aware of that. At the very beginning oft the book, he wets his pants. Nonetheless, he’s not a bad narrator. He’s the one telling the story, and it’s pleasing, that he does not know everything, does not understand everything, and acts very much like his age. He’s also a very good friend, to the point that he won’t blame his friend for things they did together, although that is what his father wants him to do. I also think, that many people can relate to him, because we all know how it feels to like someone who does not even know you exist.<br />I was not sure what tot hink about Tschick from the very beginning. I could not place him. He comes to school drunk, knows how to deal with older students, that try to make fun of him, but in the end he does not have a single friend (except for Maik). He’s originally from Russia, and managed to get to agood school in only 4 years, but if he is as intelligent as it seems, he makes no use of his gift.<br />Then there’s Isa. She is okay, flirts with Maik, and in the end the three of them get along quite well. In my opinion she’s okay, but she swears too much.<br /><br />OPINION<br />Without said lecture, I would have never read this. I don’t regret it, it was okay, but nothing special, although it was nice having a male narrator in YA. Which is my next point. The publisher did NOT intend this to be YA, but adult fiction. My teacher said it was somehow both, and it did in fact win a price for YA books. I also think it is more YA than anything else. While I can imagine younger adults (like me) reading this, I can’t picture an actual adult (like my parents) ever picking it up.<br />While I did not have any major problems with this, I quite disliked how many swear words were present. I don’t enjoy reading “piss” and “fuck” for no apparent reason (there is a reason I dislike the way teenagers talk).<br />So the story was interesting, fast to read and neither too long nor too short. It also started with the ending, so you DO want to know how Maik ended up in hospital. <br /><br />What else is there to say? I’m not sure if and to whom I’d recommend it. Read it if you like to, you probably won’t regret it. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6982157216733461981" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2180850312" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2180850312" />
    <a title="Louise / Daisy May Johnson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5230131-louise-daisy-may-johnson"><img alt="Louise / Daisy May Johnson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1554735943p2/5230131.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2180850312?book_show_action=true">Nov 11, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Louise / Daisy May Johnson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Louise / Daisy May Johnson" href="/user/show/5230131-louise-daisy-may-johnson">Louise / Daisy May Johnson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780545481809" href="/book/show/17270509-why-we-took-the-car">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5230131-louise-daisy-may-johnson?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5230131-louise-daisy-may-johnson?shelf=adventure">adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5230131-louise-daisy-may-johnson?shelf=gritty">gritty</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5230131-louise-daisy-may-johnson?shelf=coming-of-age">coming-of-age</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5230131-louise-daisy-may-johnson?shelf=travel">travel</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2180850312" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17796410513110134739">After spending time as a writer in residence for a road, I've been increasingly interested in the role of 'roads' in children's and young adult literature. Young adult literature, in fact, has a perfect sort of marriage with the metaphor of the road, where the open road promises freedom, independence and self-determination, and it's a sense of liberty which is always in sharp contrast to that which exists at home. Furiously well known in its original German, Why We Took The Car is a translated n</span>
  <span id="freeText17796410513110134739" style="display:none">After spending time as a writer in residence for a road, I've been increasingly interested in the role of 'roads' in children's and young adult literature. Young adult literature, in fact, has a perfect sort of marriage with the metaphor of the road, where the open road promises freedom, independence and self-determination, and it's a sense of liberty which is always in sharp contrast to that which exists at home. Furiously well known in its original German, Why We Took The Car is a translated novel that sometime burns with brilliance and sometimes widely misses the mark. It's a book of dualities where sadness battles with raw and fierce happiness, and nothing sometimes battles with everything. I think it is occasionally rather perfect. Sometimes it is not. But then again, that's the sort of delicious thing about roadtrip novels; there are moments, as with every journey, that the getting there matters as much as the destination itself. The journey might be quieter, duller, but it's still so very important. <br /><br />So here are our travellers: Mike, our narrator, who is a boy who doesn't fit in, and a new boy at his school called Tschick. Tschick doesn't fit in either, being an emigre from Russia, and also possessed of problems of his own. A slow twist of circumstances and parental absences lead Tschick to give Mike a dare. It's time to go on a road trip. Tschick has a stolen Lada, Mike has some money, and the open road's calling them...<br /><br />Messy, wild, eccentric, this is a book that burns on the edge of the world. I liked it a lot. It's scrappy at points, and very definitely not perfect, but then again there's a point to be made that a teenage narrator who's just had the trip of his life wouldn't ever be especially coherent. Yet that's not to say that there isn't potency here; there's an encounter with a family that is one of the best and most brilliantly unexpected things I've read with a long while, and the final movement of the book itself is kind of awe-inspiring. I think that's the best way I can describe Why We Took The Car; sometimes it is perfect, and sometimes it is not. Such is life. And sometimes, you don't know that, until you go out and live it.<br /><br />My thanks to the publisher for a review copy. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17796410513110134739" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2172907006" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2172907006" />
    <a title="Holly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/70375619-holly"><img alt="Holly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1503866069p2/70375619.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2172907006?book_show_action=true">Jan 26, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Holly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Holly" href="/user/show/70375619-holly">Holly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audible Audio " href="/book/show/31145836-tschick">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2172907006" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10723378010078413123">This book was wonderful. Flat out, hands down. It's a coming of age adventure, a couple of teenagers who haven't really found their place in the world, who are still trying to navigate the social intricacies of school, teenagedom, unrequited love, and trying to learn to grow into their own identities. The adults in their lives are largely absent. But they find one another, get hold of a beat-up old car, and set out on an adventure that takes them roaming across east Germany on country roads, col</span>
  <span id="freeText10723378010078413123" style="display:none">This book was wonderful. Flat out, hands down. It's a coming of age adventure, a couple of teenagers who haven't really found their place in the world, who are still trying to navigate the social intricacies of school, teenagedom, unrequited love, and trying to learn to grow into their own identities. The adults in their lives are largely absent. But they find one another, get hold of a beat-up old car, and set out on an adventure that takes them roaming across east Germany on country roads, colliding with strange and wonderful and wildly eccentric people as they go. Maik, the main character and first person narrator, grapples with some of his insecurities as they go, including a fascination with the fact that people just... disappear. We're here now, and people know who we are, but in a hundred years, who will think about the fact that we were here? This fear could sound totally clichéd coming from someone else's mouth, but from Maik it's not at all. Balanced against his fascination at the landscapes they're driving through and his total love and trust for his new friend Tschick, Maik's insecurities just serve to flesh him out as an engaging character. They also lend extra power to Maik's feeling of total freedom and empowerment as he and Tschick drive through new landscapes. Even just following along with them, I felt that exhilaration of exploration, heading for new horizons.<br /><br />Also, the friendship between Maik and Tschick is just beautiful. It's fun and funny and kind and respectful and loving, and it feels like way more than a clichéd "two outcasts find one another" trope; each is motivated by his own reasons to steal the car and leave on the road trip (Maik's reason is that he doesn't want to be "boring"), and the friendship develops gradually as their adventure unfolds. <br /><br />Definitely recommend as a short, thought-provoking page-turner!</span>
  <a data-text-id="10723378010078413123" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9256741790324354214"><b>
  <u>English</u>
</b><br/>A very pleasant and fast-paced book. <br/>I must admit, however, that I found the film version better (usually it’s opposite). <br/>Maik, a very unpopular boy in his school is very unhappy because he does not get an invitation to Tatjana's birthday party (his secret crush). His new friend at school, Tschick, motivates him to confront her.   <span id="freeText9256741790324354214" style="display:none"><b>
  <u>English</u>
</b><br />A very pleasant and fast-paced book. <br />I must admit, however, that I found the film version better (usually it’s opposite). <br />Maik, a very unpopular boy in his school is very unhappy because he does not get an invitation to Tatjana's birthday party (his secret crush). His new friend at school, Tschick, motivates him to confront her. To recover from this uncomfortable experience and also because there is literally nothing to do during summer holiday, these two 14-year-old boys decide to take a long drive around Germany. Throughout the book, I somehow wondered why the book is called 'Tschick'? It is explained better in the film, as without him, Maik would not have experienced such an unforgettable road trip. It makes sense now, doesn't it?<br />At the end of the book, I kind of wanted to go on a long road-trip myself.<br /><br /><b>
  <u>German</u>
</b><br />Ein sehr angenehmes und schnelllebiges Buch. <br />Ich muss allerdings zugeben, dass ich die Filmversion besser fand (normalerweise das Gegenteil). <br />Maik, ein sehr unbeliebter Junge in der Schule, ist sehr unglücklich, nachdem er keine Einladung zur Geburtstagsfeier von Tatjana (seine geheime Verliebtheit) erhalten hat. Sein neuer Freund in der Schule, Tschick, motiviert ihn, sie zu befriedigen. Um sich davon zu erholen und auch im Sommer gab es buchstäblich nichts zu tun, beschließen diese beiden 14-jährigen Jungen, eine lange Autofahrt zu unternehmen. Im ganzen Buch habe ich mich irgendwie gefragt, warum das Buch ‚Tschick‘ heißt? Im Film wird es besser erklärt, wenn man ohne Tschick sagt, dass Maik einen so unvergesslichen Roadtrip nicht erlebt hätte. Es macht jetzt Sinn, nicht wahr?<br />Am Ende des Buches wollte ich eine lange Reise machen.<br /></span>

<div id="review_3056160769" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3056160769" />
    <a title="Nilu" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4080624-nilu"><img alt="Nilu" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598930400p2/4080624.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3056160769?book_show_action=true">Nov 20, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nilu" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nilu" href="/user/show/4080624-nilu">Nilu</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10407743207336891970">This is a fabulous ‘Bildungsroman ‘ inclusive of a road trip. Those happen to be my favorite story genres and I’m lucky to have found both in one book.<br/><br/>The narrator is fourteen year old Mike from Berlin with an upper middle class upbringing , who makes friends with a Russian migrant kid named ‘Tschick’.<br/>The story revolves around an incident that occurred during a summer break.<br/><br/>Mike’s narration is witty and extremely engaging. Both him and Tschick takes the reader along the autobahn on a crazy adven</span>
  <span id="freeText10407743207336891970" style="display:none">This is a fabulous ‘Bildungsroman ‘ inclusive of a road trip. Those happen to be my favorite story genres and I’m lucky to have found both in one book.<br /><br />The narrator is fourteen year old Mike from Berlin with an upper middle class upbringing , who makes friends with a Russian migrant kid named ‘Tschick’.<br />The story revolves around an incident that occurred during a summer break.<br /><br />Mike’s narration is witty and extremely engaging. Both him and Tschick takes the reader along the autobahn on a crazy adventure . <br />Through their interactions the reader gets an impression of the East side of Germany , the growing pains of adolescence and how broken families affect the kids.<br /><br />There are moments of great hilarity and subtle heartaches, and I recommend the book to everyone who loves a good coming of age tale.</span>

<div id="review_1332868916" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1332868916" />
    <a title="Izabella (Pages Full of Stars)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4787170-izabella-pages-full-of-stars"><img alt="Izabella (Pages Full of Stars)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1582407389p2/4787170.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Izabella (Pages Full of Stars)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Izabella (Pages Full of Stars)" href="/user/show/4787170-izabella-pages-full-of-stars">Izabella (Pages Full of Stars)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" 9788377479469" href="/book/show/20323032-czik">review of another edition</a>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4787170-izabella-pages-full-of-stars?shelf=young-adult-fiction">young-adult-fiction</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4787170-izabella-pages-full-of-stars?shelf=literature-european">literature-european</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1332868916" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer79922529234386768">This was a big disappointment for me. I initially picked up this book because of its pretty cover but bought it because the story sounded interesting, however it fell completely flat. It's supposed to be a coming of age story about two 14-year olds taking a road trip across Germany but the plot is very messy, I had a hard time finding any sense or purpose in it. It already started of ridiculous when the author wanted the reader to believe that 14-year old boys could drive around without getting </span>
  <span id="freeText79922529234386768" style="display:none">This was a big disappointment for me. I initially picked up this book because of its pretty cover but bought it because the story sounded interesting, however it fell completely flat. It's supposed to be a coming of age story about two 14-year olds taking a road trip across Germany but the plot is very messy, I had a hard time finding any sense or purpose in it. It already started of ridiculous when the author wanted the reader to believe that 14-year old boys could drive around without getting noticed. I didn't really get the message he was trying to send and main characters were more and more likeable as time went by. Maybe it's going to appeal to other readers but it just wasn't a book for me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="79922529234386768" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13261466092758444453">Awesome book! I read it for the Uni. Althought I didn't enjoy really much the first half because I thought it was a little too pushed and unusual (two fourteen y/o kids stole a car and go where they want... mmmh), the second half is pure gold, perfect, awesome! I loved so much the different questions and problems they talk about.<br/>The ending was perfect, I loved it, I could read it everyday, becausa it's beautiful.<br/>I would suggest this book to anyone, mostly to the young adult ones!</span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Ew2oJwo4uy3SuTtvO9zS4aABdnJgzS1hTZcJ2xEKNpM+ip273w+t8I8tB6idAURXFdTVdL26QdHOvol07IoOnw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('mQyh4lSAWhBwqTfkkQQo2PDAgU7sjPnoj76xSvNg37W0i5R+gbdMzS09CyM32b5uRRUiSDH7lVgMlzHlDuDnuQ==')})
  }

Der Text, der sich mit seiner Bezeichnung als &quot;dramatisches Gedicht&quot; der Festlegung auf eine der dramatischen Gattungen entzieht, trug wesentlich dazu bei, den Blankvers als den klassischen deutschen Dramenvers zu etablieren. Mit seinem Nathan reagierte Lessing auf die religiöse Orthodoxie und Intoleranz seiner Zeit. Ort der Handlung ist Jerusalem während der Kreuzzüge – eine Stadt, in der Christentum, Islam und Judentum direkt aufeinandertreffen.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/952173.Wolfgang_Herrndorf">About Wolfgang Herrndorf</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/952173.Wolfgang_Herrndorf"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1366415010p3/952173.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/952173.Wolfgang_Herrndorf">Wolfgang Herrndorf
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
162
followers
</div>

</div>
